<!-- Mental State: Ecological synthesis; framing the long-term recovery process through the lens of environmental resilience -->

In ecology, there is a process called secondary succession. 
When a climax community—a fully grown, stable forest—is destroyed by a massive disturbance like a wildfire, it doesn't just grow back the way it was. The fire burns everything down to the soil. 
But the soil is still there. 
First, the pioneer species come back. Fast-growing weeds, grasses. Things that can survive in the harsh, open sunlight. Then the shrubs. Then, decades later, the big trees. 
My dad made the terrible chicken soup. He told me about a woodpecker. 
I ate the soup. I didn't throw it up. 
I think the soup is a pioneer species. I think it's the first tiny blade of grass growing in the ash. 
I am not a stable forest right now. I am a burned-out acreage. 
The fire was Mark. The fire was the balcony that wasn't there. 
But I am in the cavity of the tree. My dad is guarding the perimeter. My mom is running the triage. Mel is managing the logistics at Whitmore. 
I didn't pull the hair tie tonight. I just held it. 
It's going to take a long time to grow the big trees back. I don't know what they will look like. 
But the fire is out. 
The fire is out.

"Reyes, you are a domestic goddess and a biological marvel," Mel declares, grabbing a wine glass from the open cabinet. She walks over to the sliding glass door. "Mr. Reyes! The lights look highly professional!"

With a soft, electrical *click*, the string of Edison bulbs flares to life. They cast a warm, golden, amber glow over the wooden deck, pushing back the creeping purple shadows of the Ohio dusk. 

"Dinner is served," I call out, carrying the heavy ceramic bowl of pesto pasta out onto the deck. 

We don't eat on the floor anymore. We sit around a massive, weathered teak wood table that I bought at an estate sale and refinished myself. The string lights reflect in the wine glasses. The cicadas are humming a low, vibrating chorus in the oak tree.

Dinner on the deck. 
The pesto is from the garden. The lights are on. 
In ecology, a keystone species is an organism that helps define an entire ecosystem. Without it, the ecosystem would be dramatically different or cease to exist altogether. It holds the web together. 
Mel was my keystone species when the fire burned the forest down. Dad was the keystone species when I needed to amend the soil. Mom was the keystone species when I needed triage. 
I am looking down the table at them. 
I grew the basil. I bought the house. I made the dinner. 
I am a keystone species now. 
I am providing the shade. 
The fire is a ghost. The winter is a ghost. 
The motor is running. The flock is fed. 
We are just living.

October 11, 2042. 

The air in my backyard is sharp, dry, and forty-five degrees. The Ohio autumn has stripped the humidity from the atmosphere, leaving behind a crisp, brilliant blue sky. 

I am holding a wide, metal-tined rake in my dad's short-fingered hands. I am wearing my faded Columbus marathon hoodie and a pair of worn-in jeans. 

The massive oak tree shading my property is dropping its leaves. They cover the dormant grass in a thick, crunchy layer of russet and gold. I pull the rake toward me in long, steady, rhythmic sweeps, gathering the dead leaves into a massive pile near the back fence line, right next to the compost bin. 

`` `In a forest, nobody rakes the leaves. They fall, they decompose, and they return their carbon and nitrogen directly to the soil to feed the spring bloom. I am not throwing them away. I am just organizing the decomposition.` ``

I drag the last pile into the wire compost enclosure. I lean the rake against the fence. 

My breath plumps in small, white clouds. My shoulders ache with a dull, satisfying burn. I trace the smooth, permanent ridge of the scar inside my cheek with my tongue. It is a completely unconscious reflex, like blinking. 

I walk up the wooden stairs of the back deck. I pull the heavy door open and step into the mudroom. 

The house is quiet, smelling faintly of the coffee I brewed hours ago and the cinnamon sticks my mom left simmering on the stove yesterday. 

I toe off my boots. I walk to the kitchen sink and turn on the hot water. I use the plain, unscented bar soap, lathering my wide palms, watching the dry autumn dust swirl down the stainless steel drain. 

I dry my hands on a dish towel. I glance at the digital clock on the microwave. 

*10/11*
*11:42 AM*

<!-- Mental State: Profound, quiet realization; experiencing a historical trigger coordinate with absolute somatic neutrality and biological detachment -->
I stop wiping my hands. 

I look at the date. I look at the time. 

Eleven years ago, on this exact day, I woke up in a cinderblock dorm room with a bruised wrist, a bleeding cheek, and a shattered ecosystem. For a very long time, this specific coordinate in the calendar was a blast crater. It was a day where the gravity reversed and the air turned to poison. 

Today, I didn't even realize what day it was until I looked at the microwave. 

My heart beats a slow, rhythmic sixty beats per minute. My lungs expand easily, pulling the warm, cinnamon-scented air deep into my chest. There is no retaining wall. There is no phantom weight on my shoulders. 

`` `In human biology, cellular turnover means that almost every single cell in your body is replaced every seven to ten years. The skin, the blood, the tissue. It all dies and regenerates.` ``

I look down at my hands. I touch the skin of my bare right wrist. 

`` `I am literally, biologically, a completely different organism than the girl in the basement. He did not touch these cells. These cells belong entirely to me. They were grown in Columbus, in the sunlight, fed by Thai food and garden tomatoes and the air in this house.` ``

I walk out of the kitchen and into the living room. 

My phone is sitting on the coffee table. I pick it up. The screen is flawless, unbroken glass. I unlock it with my thumbprint. 

I open the Notes app. 

I tap the back arrow, navigating out of my current lists and into the main directory. I scroll all the way down to the bottom. 

There is a folder there. It is titled *2031*. It contains the field logs of a burned-out forest. It contains the panic, the cold stratification, the math I used to force my lungs to work when the water table dropped. 

I don't open the folder. I don't need to read the data. I already lived the experiment. 

I tap the icon to create a new, blank note.

It is October 11th. 
I was raking leaves, and I forgot. 
The cellular replacement is complete. I am made of entirely new carbon. 
In nature, when a tree falls in the woods, it sits on the forest floor. For a long time, it is a massive, physical obstacle. But eventually, the moss grows over it. The fungi break down the lignin in the wood. It softens, it crumbles, and it dissolves completely back into the topsoil. It ceases to be a fallen tree. It just becomes the earth that holds the new roots. 
The girl who went upstairs is gone. She dissolved into the topsoil a long time ago. She made the dirt rich enough for me to grow the canopy. 
I don't need to keep the fallen tree anymore. 
I am going to delete the archive. 
I am going to empty the trash. 
I am Dani Reyes. I am thirty-two years old. 
The climax community is thriving. 
The sky is blue. The air is clean. 
And my hands are my own.

October 11, 2042. 

The sun has dropped completely below the rooflines of Clintonville, leaving the backyard bathed in the crisp, indigo twilight of an Ohio autumn. 

I am sitting in a faded canvas camp chair on my back patio. The string of Edison bulbs my dad and I hung from the pergola casts a warm, amber glow over the wooden deck. In the center of the yard, contained safely within a heavy iron fire pit, a stack of split cedar logs crackles and pops, sending bright orange sparks drifting up into the cold air. 

I am wearing my oversized marathon hoodie. I am holding a paper plate. 

"I'm just saying, the structural integrity of this takeout box was compromised before it even left the restaurant," Mel argues, gesturing broadly with a plastic fork. She is sitting across the fire from me, bundled in a massive scarf, a glass of red wine balanced precariously on her knee. 

"You literally dropped it in the driveway, Hurricane," my dad rumbles from his spot on the patio sofa. He is wearing his insulated vest, thoroughly enjoying his Panang curry. "Gravity is a constant. Physics." 

"It was a micro-slip!" Mel defends, grinning as Priya rolls her eyes and hands her a napkin. 

`` `There is no retaining wall. There is no filter. I am just absorbing the light, and the heat, and the sound of my family.` ``

I take a bite from my plate. Pad see ew. Medium spice. No egg. 

It doesn't taste like survival. It doesn't taste like a cinderblock dorm room, or a frantic attempt to maintain a baseline, or the bitter fuel required to make it through a Tuesday seminar. 

It just tastes like dinner on a Saturday night in my own backyard, surrounded by the people who watered the dirt when the forest burned down. 

My mom leans over from the chair next to me and bumps her shoulder against mine. She is wearing her thick fleece jacket, off-duty and completely relaxed. She smiles at me, her dark eyes crinkling at the corners in the firelight. 

I smile back. The thick, permanent ridge of scar tissue inside my cheek pulls tight. It is just a part of my anatomy. It is the bedrock. 

I set my empty paper plate on the small side table next to me. I reach into the pocket of my hoodie. 

I pull out my phone. The screen is flawless, smooth glass. 

I open the Notes app. 

I don't look for the archive. The archive is gone. It was digested by the topsoil and turned into the garden blooming around us. I tap the icon in the bottom right corner to create a new, blank note.
